<div id="review_705182014" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/705182014" />
    <a title="Perifian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15572613-perifian"><img alt="Perifian" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/705182014?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perifian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perifian" href="/user/show/15572613-perifian">Perifian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15572613-perifian?shelf=german-giganten">german-giganten</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer705182014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10175921854741875659"><i>And Joseph took an oath of the children of Israel, saying, God will surely visit you, and ye shall carry up my bones from hence</i> <br/>— <b>Genesis 50:25</b> <br/><br/><i>And Moses took the bones of Joseph with him: for he had straitly sworn the children of Israel, saying, God will surely visit you; and ye shall carry up my bones away hence with you</i> <br/>— <b>Exodus 13:19</b> <br/><br/><i>And the bones of Joseph, which the children of Israel brought up out of Egypt, buried they in Shechem, in a parcel of ground which Jacob bought of the sons of H</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10175921854741875659" style="display:none"><i>And Joseph took an oath of the children of Israel, saying, God will surely visit you, and ye shall carry up my bones from hence</i> <br>— <b>Genesis 50:25</b> <br><br><i>And Moses took the bones of Joseph with him: for he had straitly sworn the children of Israel, saying, God will surely visit you; and ye shall carry up my bones away hence with you</i> <br>— <b>Exodus 13:19</b> <br><br><i>And the bones of Joseph, which the children of Israel brought up out of Egypt, buried they in Shechem, in a parcel of ground which Jacob bought of the sons of Hamor the father of Shechem for an hundred pieces of silver: and it became the inheritance of the children of Joseph</i> <br>— <b>Joshua 24:32</b> <br><br>Though Jacob's death and burial are Joseph's also, that's for closure of the overarching, on account of Exodus. <br><br>When structure wavered and vanished in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1402870.Women_and_Men" rel="nofollow noopener">"The Dream as Later Reported"</a>, I burst into laughter; when Tamar, tall and dark, proverbial straw, strode past Jacob and Joseph in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88076.Joseph_and_His_Brothers" rel="nofollow noopener">"The Reception"</a>, à la Akhenaten, I practically burst into tears. The unprecedented, unanticipated, emotional devastation, probably attributable to association with reception, circumstances surrounding, original presentation of source material, in contrast to exhilaration, exultation, though foudroyant, was of cumulative impact, a tower, perhaps, rather than the amorphous heap etymologically suggested, applicable, as cloudscape, to the former, inasmuch as one becomes, becomes aware. The miscellaneous components of construction and collapse, bliss, wrath, sorrow, satori sustained, accompanied ecstatic consternation: finis operis. I've felt this way several times in recent time, discovered every apple of mine eyne. <br><br>I was four, at most five, when I became completely infatuated with Ægypt. My parents bought a subscription to a magazine series at my request; I rapidly transitioned from hoarding statuettes and drawing images to learning myth and hieroglyph, reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/859889.The_Ancient_Egyptian_Pyramid_Texts" rel="nofollow noopener">The Pyramid</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/599081.The_Ancient_Egyptian_Coffin_Texts" rel="nofollow noopener">Coffin Texts</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3401616-the-egyptian-book-of-the-dead" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book of Coming Forth by Day</a>, Rosetta Stone ramification to other ancient cultures, Greece, Rome, though with preference for the Near East, the cradle of civilization, eventual incorporation of China, then the history of humanity, especially the diachronic linguistic, then neurology, cognitive science. Near the start of this epoch, I was able to travel to that land, the fondest memories of my childhood, the archaic, the modern, desert sunrise, cold limestone, Milky Way by cloudless night, Luxor, mon amour, how I would to return, so much unseen, so much unbeen, Giza, Cairo, Abu Simbel in the South, Alexandria, the North. <br><br>Partially, perhaps, on account of this palpable interest, possibly in reaction to this suffocating reaction to the recent absolute acknowledgement of mine own mortality, my mother decided to read to me the Bible, in Russian translation, nightly, Genesis to Revelation. My religious affiliation is to agnosticism, atheistic, partiality to Tao and Zen, though gauging automatic belief in both and categorizing arbitrarily, to answer inevitable multiple choice questions. My parents and their own are of a similar mind, paternal grandfather of more faith. I believe we "only" reached "Acts"; I finished several years later with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1923820.Holy_Bible?ac=1" rel="nofollow noopener">King James Version</a>, conversing with various teachers and authorities in my Catholic primary school before entering into a brief, retrospectively irrational, rebellious rejection of religion, revelling in reductionism. <br><br>I recall most strongly the story of Joseph, perhaps from particular resonance, maybe because it was early, though I was never bored, rapidity of fractal development in both senses, it was read to a very different child every day. Beyond this is that of <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=SP5EfwBWgg0" rel="nofollow noopener">Moses</a>, obviously, <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FoxZ8vliAtM" rel="nofollow noopener">Jesus</a> <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gB-F-tpy_70" rel="nofollow noopener">Christ</a>, understandably, and, ultimately, <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5hucTDV1Fvo" rel="nofollow noopener">the ultimate</a>. I would lie awake in dialogue soliloquy, debating morality, philosophy, story. Why did Joseph not instantly reveal himself to his brothers? Why did he act so bizarrely? Was this an attempt to determine who amongst them was of least conviction, thus culpability, in his death? Was this "but" a Holy Game? What were its rules? What was his relationship with God? Assumptions made of fact and fictional nature of narrative. Who wrote these words? How did they feel? How have they changed, through transcription, translation, expansion, contraction? How has their interpretation changed? This is but a fraction of the conscious ratiocination, both words variable. Although I've never quite incorporated myself into cyclical monomyth, as below, so above, so belover and over again, allusions abounding to Gilgamesh, Abraham, Christ and the modern age, characters created then were chiefly messianic orphans, immortal martyrs, living through famine, plague and war, living through death, obscurity lending legitimacy, the polymath post-human warrior, oppressed genius toppling empires, establishing anarchy, immanentizing singularity, terminal humility an improbable diagnosis. Joseph, chosen, beloved, suffered, overcame adversity, ascended, the averted sacrifice, blessing bestowed unto butchers. If Enoch could be Metatron then Joseph surely should be made Adon. The transition to the Faust myth with its completion must have seemed inevitable. <br><br>Præsumptuously contrary to the advice of John E. Woods, præeminent translator, exemplarily conserving authorial voice, certain despite only having read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53061.Death_in_Venice?ac=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Der Tod in Venedig</a> in German, I began with the "Descent into Hell". In certain sense a summation of the entire corpus, this chapter flings us into the time surrounding the period that Mann has chosen to ground our Hero's journey, interpreted as the past, the underworld, the land of Egypt, the North downriver, much as Joseph sees all as one and one as all. There is another, shorter, chapter of this form, between the final two books. Despite or, perhaps, because, of the exhaustive, yet never exhausting, description of every sense and every sense, one is immersed to such depths that the analysand takes on a contemporary, timeless character, even whilst presenting almost entirely alien Weltanschauungen, ubiquity in formally reflected numerological obsession, in infrangible conviction in absolute accuracy, time-travelling chronicler, native and God in narration, rather than novelist extraordinaire. Although the events described are rather familiar, perhaps to a somewhat lesser degree than Mann appears to expect, prior to Joseph's discovery of particular parallels and studious striving in resurrection, one is bound, soiled, alongside him in the well, maturation in retention of kiddy playfulness. Commentary, in fluctuating density, concentrating on novel, obviates, precludes much discussion, almost every decision justified, explained, mine objection to certain omissions a manifestation of personal quirks and especial interests. The reign of Akhenaten, my favourite personage of this chapter of history, along with Imhotep and Hatshepsut, inevitably mentioned, despite brevity of reign, more than Rachel, assuming realism, rather than androgynous symbolism, of representation and report, aware of proximity to inevitable affirmation of mortality , feverishly iconoclastic, attempting to establish a system for popular salvation, is incontrovertibly suitable, current obsolescence of certain constituents in recent discoveries is evidently inconsequential. I once more fail in mine original intent to provide quotations, not so much as a demonstration of style (revelation of universal irony, a triumphant warmth reminiscent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16634.The_Glass_Bead_Game" rel="nofollow noopener">Das Glasperlenspiel</a>, a favourite of my father, a fan of Mann, who claims little memory of this, I wholeheartedly agree, his magnum opus), but of intent, circumventing several obvious references to the evils of Nazism, other reviewers and clipping catastrophically, pithily: <br><br><i>Privatization does not mean reduction, and placing a high value on the self in no way implies its isolation, that it cuts itself off and hardens itself against the universal, against what is outside and above a person, in short, against everything that extends beyond the self, but in which it solemnly celebrates its recognition of itself. If, in fact, piety is the unshakeable certainty of the importance of the self, then solemn celebration is its extension, the means by which it flows into what is eternal, which then returns through it and in which it recognizes itself—which results in a loss of the self's closed-off singularity that not only does not detract from its dignity, not only is compatible with its dignity, but also enhances, indeed consecrates it.</i><br><br>Prompted by the nature of the first chapter, I decided to reacquaint myself with the Bible and visit the British museum with my mother, concentrating specifically on the Mesopotamian and, to a lesser degree, on the Ægyptian. Despite stilted formality of representation of the human figure characteristic of both, especially the latter in mathematical pluperfectitude and static uniformity, the former, comparatively modern, displayed great beauty, attention to dynamic detail and emotion, naturalistic whimsy in depiction of animals, goats, horses and, especially, lions. The following lioness is from a Jörmungandrian frieze from the North Palace of Ashurbanipal: <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1387650668i/7642753._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="250" alt="The Dying Lioness" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Many were depicted, hedgehogged with arrows, vomiting blood, expressions of extreme agony, a demonstration of cruelty and its abhorrence. I cannot find examples of the previous, apparently they aren't quite as appealing to photographers. My mother had several relatives from Iran, feeling a strong connection to the ancient art and culture of it and its immediate surroundings, contributing to the colossal impact of this work of eloquently elegant profundity. I have now read the last, my favourite, of Mann's greatest works, the only one in this translation, I intend to do so with the others also; comparison, contrast with them, shall follow. It is miniscule in its enormity despite enormity of scope, subverting certain stereotypes, reactionary moted veil of light about abject atrocity wrapped enrapt, I approach the notoriously pessimistic <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/527756.The_Man_Without_Qualities" rel="nofollow noopener">Mann ohne Eigenschaften</a> with some apprehension, even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88077.The_Magic_Mountain" rel="nofollow noopener">Der Zauberberg</a>, partially preoccupied with death, bursts with life. I shall do mine absolute utmost to engender interest in this inexplicably, though explicably, neglected quartet, this truly indescribably great work of art. <br><br><b>Review in Progress</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="10175921854741875659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/243285328" />
    <a title="Friedrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7115533-friedrick"><img alt="Friedrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379267671p2/7115533.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/243285328?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Friedrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Friedrick" href="/user/show/7115533-friedrick">Friedrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7115533-friedrick?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer243285328" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6510252013374118048">I've lost count of the number of times I've read Mann's magnum opus. It is enormous, sustaining, and deeply satisfying on scales both immense and minute, no matter how many times you read it. It goes much further than Paradise Lost toward fulfilling Milton's purpose to "justify the ways of God to men." I believe it is the best novel ever written in any language.<br/><br/>There now have been two English translations of this great masterpiece. H.T. Lowe-Porter's was done contemporanously with the four-part </span>
  <span id="freeText6510252013374118048" style="display:none">I've lost count of the number of times I've read Mann's magnum opus. It is enormous, sustaining, and deeply satisfying on scales both immense and minute, no matter how many times you read it. It goes much further than Paradise Lost toward fulfilling Milton's purpose to "justify the ways of God to men." I believe it is the best novel ever written in any language.<br /><br />There now have been two English translations of this great masterpiece. H.T. Lowe-Porter's was done contemporanously with the four-part original publication in German, from 1936 to 1944. A single-volume edition of her translation came out in 1948 and remained in print into the 1990s. John E. Woods published his in Everyman's Library in 2005. Lately Lowe-Porter has come under considerable popular criticism for her translations of Mann's works, particularly for her censorship of Death in Venice. Her translation of Joseph and His Brothers has been criticized for her use of phrasing and cadences reminiscent of the King James Bible that don't occur in the original German and which Woods avoided. Nevertheless, it was through Lowe-Porter's version that I first read and loved this book, and while it is Woods's translation that I now read, it is from Lowe-Porter's that the lines come spontaneously to mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6510252013374118048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3225217447" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3225217447" />
    <a title="Lia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44005555-lia"><img alt="Lia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578446760p2/44005555.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3225217447?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lia" href="/user/show/44005555-lia">Lia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44005555-lia?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3225217447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer641527038439111929">The “game” metaphor at the end is both excellent and baffling. It’s incredible that one can dismiss and forgive so much hate and rancor and revenge just by calling it a game, and it’s possible only from the POV of someone who seems to exist outside time, so that 13 years here, 7 years there, 3 days in a pit, are all irrelevant, nothing, like Athena’s clueless response to Odysseus’ accusation that she had abandoned him.<br/><br/>And maybe that’s how enmity can be set aside at all: not confronted, but dismi</span>
  <span id="freeText641527038439111929" style="display:none">The “game” metaphor at the end is both excellent and baffling. It’s incredible that one can dismiss and forgive so much hate and rancor and revenge just by calling it a game, and it’s possible only from the POV of someone who seems to exist outside time, so that 13 years here, 7 years there, 3 days in a pit, are all irrelevant, nothing, like Athena’s clueless response to Odysseus’ accusation that she had abandoned him.<br /><br />And maybe that’s how enmity can be set aside at all: not confronted, but dismissed. To have a view that encompasses everything, every time scale, every tribe, every custom, so that even rancorous feuds are mere games, a small obstacle on way to fulfilling something “good.”<br /><br />Overall an enjoyable book, I especially love the crossing of various myths and traditions and rituals in the detailed reimagining, so that Joseph might as well be Gilgamesh, and the terrible, tyrannical patriarch is also a rascal, a trickster. <br /><br />The idea of reducing treachery into mere gambits is wonderful, and curious, but it is not without problems. I do have problems with Mann characterizing or whitewashing some of the more heinous deeds (or at least rumors) as mere exchange in tricks (rape, enslavement), or harmless rough-and-tumble play between brothers. <br /><br />I still can’t believe I finished all 1492 pages of this wonderful book! It’s both too long, and not enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="641527038439111929" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1098302388" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1098302388" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1098302388?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=german-literature">german-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=bible">bible</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1098302388" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3435026551085575217">It took me three months to digest this gigantic work, 10 to 20 pages a day. So, inevitably notions like "monumental, epic, awesome", come to mind. Thomas Mann has developed the rather short Bible story about Isaac, Jacob and Joseph into 4 books, 1300 pages in total. <br/>It’s the epic strength of the story and also the setting (especially ancient Egypt) that give this book its quality. Mann has written some really moving parts, most of all those about the interaction between Joseph and his father Jac</span>
  <span id="freeText3435026551085575217" style="display:none">It took me three months to digest this gigantic work, 10 to 20 pages a day. So, inevitably notions like "monumental, epic, awesome", come to mind. Thomas Mann has developed the rather short Bible story about Isaac, Jacob and Joseph into 4 books, 1300 pages in total. <br />It’s the epic strength of the story and also the setting (especially ancient Egypt) that give this book its quality. Mann has written some really moving parts, most of all those about the interaction between Joseph and his father Jacob or Jacob and his wife Rachel; and some parts were quite funny too, like Jacob becoming victim of his own cunning. In general, I was very impressed by the profile of Jacob, represented by Mann as a real patriarch. And also the theological framework (God expressing himself in the story of man, in mutual interdepence) was pretty much inspiring.<br />But Mann has put into the book some rather bizarre interpretations, as for instance the influence of Joseph on pharaoh Ikhnaton (suggesting that Joseph made him change his worship of the sun disc into something more monotheistic), and the Christological references (Joseph seeing his adventure in the pit and in the Egyptian prison as a kind of death and a prelude to resurrection); maybe it is just Mann’s way to add some irony, but to me the anachronisms were rather disagreeable.<br />As lots of other readers I sometimes was exasperated by the very extensive way of writing and describing, with long, rather tiresome enumerations that add poorly to the story. Also Mann’s continuous toning down of the story (his own brand of irony?) conflict with the omniscient position of the author. <br />And then there is the character of Joseph; it took me a while to realize why I did not succeed in sympathizing with him (on the contrary), until Mann himself, around page 1000, characterized the actions of Joseph as rather arrogant, egocentric and without empathy. Mann adds we cannot but forgive Joseph because his actions are provoked by his sense (rather certainty) to be blessed (by this father and by God), to be destined, what makes everything he does and has to undergo into a part of a much broader, divine story. Well, at that point I knew: if I would come across Joseph in real life, I certainly would find him abhorrent and odious, like all people I meet that are 200% certain of their own convictions, their special part in history, no matter if it is on religious, philosophical, ideological or plain materialistic grounds. Perhaps it's a kind of biblical irony when in the end not Joseph appears to be the chosen one, but the modest and sinful Juda.<br />I agree, Mann his written a monumental work, but I cannot really sympathize with it, nor with Joseph. I prefer the ever seeking, uncertain Hans Castorp of Magic Mountain, still one of the most important books of the 20th Century!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3435026551085575217" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2689687263" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2689687263" />
    <a title="Jef Sneider" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/122610-jef-sneider"><img alt="Jef Sneider" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187905464p2/122610.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2689687263?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jef Sneider" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jef Sneider" href="/user/show/122610-jef-sneider">Jef Sneider</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122610-jef-sneider?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122610-jef-sneider?shelf=israel-middle-east">israel-middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122610-jef-sneider?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122610-jef-sneider?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122610-jef-sneider?shelf=spiritual-explorations">spiritual-explorations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/122610-jef-sneider?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2689687263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6879773957846729194">Stunning! And, you know the story! You have read the biblical version and heard the commentaries, but you have never heard the story told in this way. Jacob, Rachael. Young Joseph. The land from Syria to Egypt. The sun. The moon. The stars. The planets. The constellations. Leah, her heavy lidded eyes. Her fecundity. But the writing. The reality, emotions and causes. I am in awe of the writer. My mouth hangs open as I read. And, in the end, the end of the first book, I cried. I cried as Jacob cri</span>
  <span id="freeText6879773957846729194" style="display:none">Stunning! And, you know the story! You have read the biblical version and heard the commentaries, but you have never heard the story told in this way. Jacob, Rachael. Young Joseph. The land from Syria to Egypt. The sun. The moon. The stars. The planets. The constellations. Leah, her heavy lidded eyes. Her fecundity. But the writing. The reality, emotions and causes. I am in awe of the writer. My mouth hangs open as I read. And, in the end, the end of the first book, I cried. I cried as Jacob cried over the loss of his beloved Rachael, age 41, who died during childbirth. Poor Dinah. I read her story first, then started “Stories of Jacob,” the first book, and then back to the introduction - Descent into Hell - as suggested by the translator, and the first chapter, At the Well, which is the most beautiful of them all, giving a taste of what a writer is capable of. The first book has ended: three more remain to delight, enlighten and entertain. I shall read on!<br /><br />The second book is “Young Joseph,”and the beautiful and engaging writing style continues. As the story unfolds, the author struggles along with us to fill in the details of a story well known. How can one write with suspense or tension when everyone, writer and reader, knows how it will turn out. “The story tells itself” in real time, he writes even as we read the re-telling and the author writes it. <br /><br />Joseph’s love for his little brother, Benjamin, and their lives together form the most beautiful part of this book. While the dialogue between the two has the vocabulary of a professor of English and the complexity of a Shakespeare verse, the feeling between the two and their bond, as two children with the same mother, makes palpable a real relationship and their love for one another. Benjamin’s unfortunate guilt for the death of his mother in childbirth is heartrending.<br /><br />If you read nothing else, read the chapter called, "The Lesson." Young Joseph's work in the fields and as a shepherd did not fill all his time. He was, after all, the spoiled "child of the one true wife," and he had time for other activities. For one thing, he gets an education in the known science and mathematics of the age from his tutor, Eliezer, for whom "the earth had lept up to greet him" as he sought Rebeccah at the well for Isaac. The story takes place 3000 years ago. What did they know of the planets and mathematics, and what could the aging servant of a man like Jacob teach his precocious son? I was surprised! In beautiful and engaging language the author takes us on a trip through the planets and the calendar, through numbers and their numerical and spiritual meanings, every part of the lesson laced with knowledge of God and history. This is my favorite chapter in the book.<br /><br />Of course, we all know where this story is going as Joseph manages to get Jacob to give him the famous many colored garment; in this case a lavishly embroidered garment worn by Rachael when she was wedded with Jacob, hence the garment of Joseph’s mother, but also the garment bestowing honor and blessing upon the first son of the one true wife. To the other brothers, the ten other than Benjamin and Joseph, this seems to be an obvious blessing bestowed upon Joseph as it comes on the heels of the disgrace of Reuben, first born of Leah, who is punished, losing his blessing as the first born, for the indiscretion of sleeping with Jacob’s concubine and Leah’s servant, Bilhah. <br /><br />This cannot end well, and it does not. It is torture to read about Joseph’s fall, his decent into the pit and his pitiful state as the brothers leave him to die and then ultimately sell him into slavery. Every detail is wrought with care, obviously after much research and creative thought. In the end, the retelling of a story whose ending we already know still leaves us awed and breathless.<br /><br />The third book is “Joseph in Egypt.” I can believe that it took Mann over 20 years to write this collection of four books retelling the biblical story because the detail describing Joseph’s entry into Egypt and the Egyptian gods, their culture and traditions, the details of their economy and their political structures is overwhelming. We know what happens. Joseph is blessed and eventually becomes the chief steward to Potiphar, a favored friend of the Pharoah. <br /><br />In the biblical version there are just a few verses which then describe how Potiphar’s wife tries to seduce Joseph into lying with her. When he refuses, she becomes incensed, rips his tunic from his shoulders as he tries to run away and uses that as evidence that he tried to force her into submission. Once again, a garment is Joseph’s downfall; again his own tunic, and if Jacob believes that Joseph is dead when he sees the bloody many colored garment, Potiphar has to believe that Joseph is guilty when he is shown the visible proof of the matter.<br /><br />A simple story, right? Well, not to Thomas Mann. In these few biblical verses, Thomas Mann sees a challenge. How can one explain the relationship between the wife of a powerful Egyptian vassle of the Pharoah and a lowly Hebrew slave? In his response to this conundrum, the author give an extended answer, entering the mind of the beautiful Mut-en-enet, first wife and most elevated woman in the harem as well as a singer and dancer and priestess of the god Amun. Never was an unrequited love explored in all its mystery and painful development than here, taking up the majority of the book describing Joseph’s life in Egypt before he is thrown into the pit for a second time, again betrayed by a garmnent of clothing, the first of which he should not have worn and the second of which he would have done better top keep on his back!<br /><br />And, we know what is going to happen all along, making the author’s extended exposition frustrating and exasperating. Finally, aware of our pain even as he enjoys continuing to drag this story out in exquisite detail, he writes, “The audience’s [that would be us, the readers] impatience to be told what everyone knows has doubtless reached its peak by now.” And the reader responds, yes! It has! I know how this will end, just give me the details!<br /><br />The final denoumont is done well and Potiphar acts with surprising intelligence and understanding as he assigns Joseph to Pharoah’s prison, “for you will no longer belong to me, but to Pharaoh and are a slave of the king. “ Ahah! And we know where that leads: Book four, “Joseph the Provider.”<br /><br />The final book of the set chronicles Joseph's rise from prison to be the steward of Egypt's agrarian economy, and his eventual reunification with his brothers and his father. Here the author faces another challenge. The bible simply reads that after Joseph's interpretation of his dreams, Pharaoh recognizes Joseph's brilliance, saying, "there is none so discreet and wise as thou" and chooses him to be the overseer of the strategy to save Egypt, and much of the known world, from famine. How could this happen? How could Pharaoh, who is a god himself, surrounded by a court and advisors, make such a choice, impulsively and in the course of a single day? <br /><br />Like the story of Potiphar's wife and her infatuation with the slave from the west, Mann chooses here to expound and and expand the conversation between Joseph and Pharaoh (and Pharaoh's mother the royal consort who had ruled Egypt until her son achieved the necessary age and maturity). Pharaoh is not interested in the management of the empire. As Pharaoh himself responds to Joseph's request for 70 days leave to bury his father in his homeland, he is concerned about the difficulty he will have managing the land in the time of famine without Joseph, saying "My Majesty understands little of all that - my interest has always been in the light above."<br /><br />So, here we digress and expound and expand into a very interesting discussion of that "light above," for Pharaoh himself is not only a god and a man, he is the son of a god, for wasn't Pharaoh's deceased father also Pharaoh and also a god, now in Heaven? So he discusses the meaning of being a god and the son of a god with the spirit of god and references to a threefold godhead, intertwined with Joseph's own twice death and resurrection, once into the pit and once into prison, and the names of both Joseph and Pharaoh which change during their lives. This takes a while, but revolves and the story goes, in favor of Jospeh. These discussions, as with many other passages in the book, presage much of the religious conversations and writings to come thousands of years later. <br /><br />Only after this long conversation is the author finally able to allow the young Pharaoh, over the objections of his mother, of course, "with a boy's exuberant eagerness" and a clap of his hands, to name Joseph as the "Pharaoh's Supreme Mouth, his Grand Vizier, Friend of God's Harvest, Spender of Shade to the King and Father of Pharaoh, Prince of the Interior and Vice-God, and Pharaoh's Unique Friend."<br /><br />From here the story tears ahead, as though Mann's 20 year journey from Germany to Switzerland to Princeton and eventually California was tumbling forward with greater and greater speed drawing the story of Joseph with him, through the 5 years of surplus and plenty, or was it seven? And is there a difference to people when things are going well - who's counting? And into the lean years, the story of Jacob and his sons, the tricks and devices used by Joseph to finally see his brother from the one true wife, Benjamin, the reconciliation and meeting with his father, Jacob, as the entire family, numbering 70 souls (or more), moves from Hebron in the land of Canaan to the fertile pastures of Goshen in the Two Lands of Egypt, of Menassah and Ephraim and most especially to explain Joseph's future place in the history of the people and the story of Abraham, Isaac and Jacob. <br /><br />Foretelling Moses, who will not be allowed to enter into the promised land, Joseph has been "set apart" from his people, and the future of people shall not be through him, but through his brothers and especially the line of Judah. There is no tribe of Joseph, but Jacob adopts Ephraim and Menassah to carry on Joseph's line for him.<br /><br />All of this is told in beautiful language, with grace and dignity, and with a wry sense of awareness that it is a story and that we all know what will happen, and I read the conversations between Joseph and Jacob and his brothers with tears even though I also knew the whole story, how it started and how it ended and who would live and who would die.<br /><br />One last comment: Through all the book, the origin stories of Adam and Eve and Cain and Abel and Noah and his sons and Abraham the Chaldean from Ur, and the almost sacrificed Isaac and the servant, Eliezer for whom the earth had leapt up to greet him, and the trickery between Rebeccah and Isaac and Jacob and Esau and all the stories of Genesis are repeated in one beautiful way after another, over and over. At the end of 1492 pages one can understand how the repetition of such stories over generations would create a real, yet stylized version of the greater story that has become the bible. Thomas Mann tries to bring this story back to "the story as it happened" - filling out the characters and the action so that we can understand how these things could have happened and how these lives were lived, so that now we all know the story, how it started and how it ended, and who lived and who died.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6879773957846729194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3461699032" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3461699032" />
    <a title="Thomas Guidotti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71643871-thomas-guidotti"><img alt="Thomas Guidotti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556234515p2/71643871.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3461699032?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas Guidotti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas Guidotti" href="/user/show/71643871-thomas-guidotti">Thomas Guidotti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71643871-thomas-guidotti?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3461699032" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18107278704490984408">This took me a while but that's not because it was boring in any way whatsoever, rather the book is a really stunning accomplishment in modern literature that demands a thorough and attentive reading. After all it took Thomas Mann 16 years to complete the novel. It is criminal that when people discuss Thomas Mann's works this book is extremely overlooked because he himself viewed it as his best work and after reading it myself I would say that it is hard to argue otherwise. This book is the lite</span>
  <span id="freeText18107278704490984408" style="display:none">This took me a while but that's not because it was boring in any way whatsoever, rather the book is a really stunning accomplishment in modern literature that demands a thorough and attentive reading. After all it took Thomas Mann 16 years to complete the novel. It is criminal that when people discuss Thomas Mann's works this book is extremely overlooked because he himself viewed it as his best work and after reading it myself I would say that it is hard to argue otherwise. This book is the literary equivalent of the music of Wagner in the sense that it is a story of recurring motifs playing themselves out over and over again in the lives of Jacob and Joseph. These characters identify themselves with the myths of their forefathers and they see these stories playing out in their own lives. When Jacob thinks of the story of Abraham leading his people to Egypt during a time of famine (the "descent into the underworld", a recurring motif) he identifies his fleeing from Esau's wrath after cheating him out of his birthright as a recurrence of this same theme in his own life. When Joseph is sold into slavery by his brothers and descends into Egypt he clearly associates this story with Jacob's sojourn in Mesopotamia with Laban. From the beginning of the novel Mann asks the readers to think about whether the stories and myths we are told of the heavens above are a reflection of our own lives on earth or rather is it the other way around. In the preface to the novel Mann discusses the presence of the myth of the flood in every early culture, including the Biblical story of the flood, the story of the flood in the epic of Gilgamesh, and early Chinese tradition. Is this just a story that somehow arose independently in each culture or is it a reflection of some ancient primal flood that has been preserved through thousands of years of oral tradition? <br /><br />Another really key theme to this novel is the contrast between monotheism and polytheism. Every other character outside of the tribe of Israel identifies themselves with their own gods and Jacob and Joseph's conflicting views play a central part of the narrative. Probably my favorite scene in the entire book comes around a third of the way into the fourth book when Joseph and Pharaoh discuss the nature of Aton in contrast to the Hebrew God. Pharaoh (and the Egyptians in general) identifies his gods with signs made manifest to human eyes, whether that means the sun, the moon, the stars, or animals. When Joseph challenges this idea with the belief that even the god that Pharaoh believes is behind these images is but itself a mere shadow of his own God Pharaoh begins to question the nature of his own belief. The idea that even if you were to glimpse the shadow of the things that we see with our own eyes that there is another shadow that lies beyond is the other key to the novel. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18107278704490984408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_43410379" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/43410379" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/426277-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584954318p2/426277.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43410379?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/426277-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394431321" href="/book/show/1322819.Joseph_and_His_Brothers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=read-and-reread">read-and-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=top-twenty">top-twenty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=mitteleuropa">mitteleuropa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=sunday-morning">sunday-morning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/426277-james?shelf=nobel-prize-author">nobel-prize-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer43410379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2795197191560070475">"Very deep is the well of the past. Should we not call it bottomless?"<br/>With this beginning Thomas Mann creates a monumental novel based on the story of Joseph in Genesis. By the time you have read more than two hundred pages and Joseph is yet to be born you begin to realize just how monumental this novel will be. The good news is that it is worth the time and effort. <br/>Mann sets the story in the 14th century BC and makes Akhenaten the pharaoh who makes Joseph his vice-regent. A dominant topic of th</span>
  <span id="freeText2795197191560070475" style="display:none">"Very deep is the well of the past. Should we not call it bottomless?"<br />With this beginning Thomas Mann creates a monumental novel based on the story of Joseph in Genesis. By the time you have read more than two hundred pages and Joseph is yet to be born you begin to realize just how monumental this novel will be. The good news is that it is worth the time and effort. <br />Mann sets the story in the 14th century BC and makes Akhenaten the pharaoh who makes Joseph his vice-regent. A dominant topic of the novel is Mann's exploration of the status of mythology and his presentation of mythical truths and the emergence of monotheism. Events of the story of Genesis are frequently associated and identified with other mythic topics.<br />From the opening page of the novel the notion of underworld and the mythical descent to the underworld is a central theme. Jacob's sojourn in Mesopotamia (hiding from the wrath of Esau) is paralleled with Joseph's life in Egypt (exiled by the jealousy of his brothers), and on a smaller scale his captivity in the well. Abraham is repeatedly presented as the man who "discovered God". Jacob as Abraham's heir is charged with further elaborating this discovery. Joseph is surprised to find Akhenaten on the same path (although Akhenaten is not the "right person" for the path), and Joseph's success with the pharaoh is largely due to the latter's sympathy for "Abrahamic" theology. Mann's approach reminds one of Sigmund Freud's Moses and Monotheism, which had appeared in 1939, just before Mann began work on the tetralogy's fourth part. As Joseph is saved from the well and sold to Egypt, he adopts a new name, Osarseph, replacing the Yo- element with a reference to Osiris to indicate that he is now in the underworld. This change of name to account for changing circumstances encourages Amenhotep to change his own name to Akhenaten.<br />The breadth of the story can be seen in a survey of the four major sections of the novel. The first, The Tales of Jacob, recounts the story of the birthright struggle with Esau and Dinah's trials. When it finally introduces us to the relationship between Jacob and his favored son, Joseph, first-born of his favored wife Rachel, you see one whose flaws of over-confidence and presumption are obvious from the beginning. The familiar stories here include how Jacob’s mother, Rebeccca, managed to obtain Isaac’s blessing for Jacob rather than first-born Esau (the “clod”) and the parallel deception by Jacob’s host Laban in which Jacob was given Leah’s hand in marriage rather than Rachel’s. The second part, Young Joseph, tells the familiar story of Joseph’s catastrophic descent in which his brothers, utterly fed up with his arrogance, throw him into the pit from which he is rescued three days later by an itinerant group of merchants. Having sold him to the merchants, his brothers then deceive their father by showing him the many-colored coat, stained with the blood of a lamb and leaving it to him to draw the conclusion that his son was killed by a lion. <br />The two remaining sections take Joseph to Egypt and the court of the Pharaoh through to his reunion with his father and brothers. Mann's recounting of the story of Potiphar and his wife is exceptional among the many stories that are related about Joseph's life in Egypt. You also see Joseph growing in wisdom although he is not always aware of his own abilities. "Toward him alone and urgently went Joseph's thoughts and the speech of his tongue, unaware that it was guided not by chance or choice but by inheritance and tradition."(p 459) <br />Mann is a fascinating, extraordinarily well-read writer with a powerful understanding of the human condition. His character studies (and Joseph is nothing if not a very extended character study) are deep and multi-faceted. He does a remarkable job of bring to life a pre-montheistic way of thinking. His thoughtfulness and imagination combine to create an immense classic that wins you over time and again. The depth of this remarkable classic knows no bounds. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2795197191560070475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1180048154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1180048154" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/803405-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1278110150p2/803405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1180048154?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/803405-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1180048154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1136434883445861593">Yes, Abram had knowingly shared his high optimism with his family. He was called Abiram, which may have meant "my father is exalted," or, quite rightly and probably, "father of the Exalted." For in some measure Abraham was God's father. He had discovered Him and thought Him into being. Those mighty attributes that he ascribed to Him were surely God's original property, Abram was not their originator. But by recognizing them, teaching them, and realizing them in his own thought, was he not His fa</span>
  <span id="freeText1136434883445861593" style="display:none">Yes, Abram had knowingly shared his high optimism with his family. He was called Abiram, which may have meant "my father is exalted," or, quite rightly and probably, "father of the Exalted." For in some measure Abraham was God's father. He had discovered Him and thought Him into being. Those mighty attributes that he ascribed to Him were surely God's original property, Abram was not their originator. But by recognizing them, teaching them, and realizing them in his own thought, was he not His father in a certain sense? God's powerful attributes were, to be sure, a given reality outside of Abraham, but at the same time they were also in him and from him; at certain moments the power of his own soul -- deliberately shrinking and melting into one with them -- was scarcely to be differentiated from them. Here lay the origin of the covenant that the Lord made with Abraham and that was merely the explicit confirmation of an inner reality; this was also, however, the origin of the peculiar quality of Abram's fear of God. For since God's greatness was indeed something terrible and real outside him and yet at the same time coincided in some sense with his own soul and was indeed its product, Abram's fear of God was not fear alone in the true sense of the word -- it was not only trembling and quaking, but also attachment, intimacy, and friendship, all in one. And at times the first father did indeed have a way of dealing with God that, if one did not take into consideration the interlocking peculiarity of their relationship, must have aroused amazement in heaven and earth. For example, given God's terrible power and greatness, Abram's friendly rebuke of the Lord during the destruction of Sodom and Amorrah had verged on being offensive. But, to be sure, whom should it offend if not God? And He had taken it well enough. "Hear me, Lord," Abram had said on that occasion, "yes or no, one way or the other! If You want to have a world, You cannot demand justice; but if You care only for justice, then the world is done for. You grasp the cord by both ends, wanting both a world and justice in it. But if You are not more lenient, the world cannot stand." He had even accused the Lord of deception and reproached Him for having once sworn never to flood the world again, and yet here He came with a flood of fire. But God, who surely could not have dealt any differently with these cities after what had happened, or almost happened, to His messengers, had received it all, if not with good, at least not with ill will; to all of it He had wrapped Himself in benevolent silence.<br /><br />This silence was the expression of a monstrous fact that belonged both to God's external presence and at the same time to the largeness of Abram's soul, which was perhaps its most proper source: the fact that the contradiction of a living world that was also supposed to be just resided within God's greatness itself. For He, the living God, was not good or merely good among other things, but was evil besides. His living presence embraced evil and was at the same time holy, was holiness in and of itself, holiness that demanded holiness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1136434883445861593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3491716063524264738">The first two parts were cult material for me and put this book up alongside Dostoyevsky and Moby Dick in my five most significant. Less so once in Egypt. I imagine that is partly me: my mind was ignited by little chapters such as 'The Red One' and 'The Primordial Bleating' where, for want of a way to phrase this, we get positively atavistic; Egypt became more of a conventional historical novel, and besides, I myself loved the tent life, the sheep and wells, and the archaic portions of the Bible</span>
  <span id="freeText3491716063524264738" style="display:none">The first two parts were cult material for me and put this book up alongside Dostoyevsky and Moby Dick in my five most significant. Less so once in Egypt. I imagine that is partly me: my mind was ignited by little chapters such as 'The Red One' and 'The Primordial Bleating' where, for want of a way to phrase this, we get positively atavistic; Egypt became more of a conventional historical novel, and besides, I myself loved the tent life, the sheep and wells, and the archaic portions of the Bible -- not smooth civilized Egypt. I was under twenty and never had the stamina to finish. Last year I flipped through the second half and honest to God was not impressed. <br /><br />I read the H.T. Lowe-Porter and lapped up the biblical style but thought Mann grew increasingly diffuse, to less purpose as we dwell in Egypt. From this review <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.newrepublic.com/article/flesh-and-myth">http://www.newrepublic.com/article/fl...</a> (translation notes near the end) I see the diffuseness may not be Mann's. Shall have to go back to this with the John E. Woods translation.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1369513334" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1369513334" />
    <a title="Jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9988962-jeremy"><img alt="Jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486326794p2/9988962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1369513334?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/9988962-jeremy">Jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1369513334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7086354810370317660">A monumental feat of historical and biblical world building, Joseph achieves its ambitious goal of 'realizing' the incredulous lives of Jacob and his sons. Painstaking research and details make it seem as though Mann used a time machine, because he certainly wasn't able to use the internet. At times the density, with multitudes of names in different tongues of old gods, can confound, and the last part in Egypt can be a slog, unless of course you're an ancient Egyptian religion and culture schola</span>
  <span id="freeText7086354810370317660" style="display:none">A monumental feat of historical and biblical world building, Joseph achieves its ambitious goal of 'realizing' the incredulous lives of Jacob and his sons. Painstaking research and details make it seem as though Mann used a time machine, because he certainly wasn't able to use the internet. At times the density, with multitudes of names in different tongues of old gods, can confound, and the last part in Egypt can be a slog, unless of course you're an ancient Egyptian religion and culture scholar. <br /><br />As long and slow as this epic seems, at times it is quite thrilling: Joseph's brothers murderous rage, Joseph's ascendancy and fall at the residence of Potiphar, and Joseph rising from a prison slave to the second most powerful man in Egypt over the course of an hour. The ladder is pulled off quite nicely and should please fans of the story in the Bible who would like the whole story fleshed out. It's surprising more writers haven't conducted similar biblical exercises, but it did take Mann decades to finish this. In the end, he calls his story "an invention of God", as though the story, no matter how 'real' it may be, is of divine import. <br /><br />(**The John E. Woods translation cannot be praised enough. It is a towering achievement jeweled with beautiful sentences.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7086354810370317660" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer106056446" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer794088812761752314">This is some dense prose. Mann really gives his language a biblical feel, though still modernized. It's really thick and can get a little exhausting, but it is also extremely beautiful language. It's an enthralling retelling. Although, I thought this was a story I was pretty familiar with and I come to find out some of what was new to me wasn't even stuff Mann made up. Mann does a great job of seamlessly integrating some of his invention with that of the bible. You could have a game show "Is it </span>
  <span id="freeText794088812761752314" style="display:none">This is some dense prose. Mann really gives his language a biblical feel, though still modernized. It's really thick and can get a little exhausting, but it is also extremely beautiful language. It's an enthralling retelling. Although, I thought this was a story I was pretty familiar with and I come to find out some of what was new to me wasn't even stuff Mann made up. Mann does a great job of seamlessly integrating some of his invention with that of the bible. You could have a game show "Is it God or is it Mann?" Then, at other points that are so much more clearly Mann you get some really interesting doubts on points from the bible story, application of more comprehensive myth cycles and archetypes, and all sorts of other goodies. It's a definite must read, but does take some effort.</span>

  <span id="freeText11064656050017495665" style="display:none">This is one of the most wonderfull books ever writen, no doubt. The story of the bible is the point of departure for a beautiful analysis of humanity, full of humour and grandeur. The book is big and one has to read it carefully to enjoy it completely. Every sentence is a jewel, every passage is full of simple life elements that wonder and links us to the past to a point were we conclude that being human is a universal experience, independent of time and space. This is all blended in with a carefull historical research, a detailed reading of the Bible and of the sacred texts. A masterpiece at its fully extent that is curiously not that popular in the english language. </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer85167287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16231989674473104501">An incredible achievement! Mann considered this his best work and I agree. While not a quick or easy read, the rhythms carry you along page after page and in my case month after month. I savored the writing while enjoying Mann's extended exegesis on time, myth, story telling, history, religion, belief, etc. etc. What a shame this is not better known although the new translation should help considerably.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

No. Does it help me to understand the Joseph story in new and fascinating ways; does it have moments of true glory; can you skim huge chunks without missing anything of any importance whatsoever? Yes. </span>

<div id="review_3570717638" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3570717638" />
    <a title="Paul Jellinek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2942842-paul-jellinek"><img alt="Paul Jellinek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298389715p2/2942842.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3570717638?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Jellinek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Jellinek" href="/user/show/2942842-paul-jellinek">Paul Jellinek</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3570717638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4107103332627010098">The father of one of my best friends told me, a few years before he died, that Joseph and His Brothers was the greatest novel of the 20th century, and even though it was 1,500 pages long, he strongly urged me to read it. I had a lot of respect for my friend's father and his judgment, so I bought the book--and there it sat on my shelf, until finally, six months ago, the pandemic struck and I suddenly found myself with time on my hands. So I pulled the book from the shelf and plunged in, finally r</span>
  <span id="freeText4107103332627010098" style="display:none">The father of one of my best friends told me, a few years before he died, that Joseph and His Brothers was the greatest novel of the 20th century, and even though it was 1,500 pages long, he strongly urged me to read it. I had a lot of respect for my friend's father and his judgment, so I bought the book--and there it sat on my shelf, until finally, six months ago, the pandemic struck and I suddenly found myself with time on my hands. So I pulled the book from the shelf and plunged in, finally re-emerging six months later (I did read a number other books along the way). Whether it is THE greatest book of the 20th century is of course debatable, but it's certainly right up there among the top ten. Mann digs deep into human history and the human psyche as he re-tells the Old Testament stories, not only of Joseph and his brothers, but of Joseph's father Jacob and HIS brother (Esau), and THEIR father (Isaac), and on and on. Using a blend of humor and detailed Biblical imagery, he brings them and their world (including the glories of ancient Egypt) to life, and, by extension, brings the entire Old Testament (and, by further extension, the New Testament) to life in a way that no other book I've ever read--including the Bible itself--does. It is worth noting, too, that Mann began working on this magnum opus in Germany in the early 1930's and completed it in Los Angeles in the 1940's. In other words, this story of the origins of Judaism was written against the backdrop of the rise and fall of the Third Reich, so that it can be read--and may very well have been intended--as Mann's supremely eloquent and magnificent rebuttal to Hitler and all that he stood for. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4107103332627010098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zS7oDmPHRewP6Cdq5kVd6R0x9Dolq+q6YI3JN5oFErSfczTBUQ0pQ9Ub9zkAP2kV9Eu9fsYPmdg8+KPsWaf0/A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Here is their story as re-imagined by Thomas Pynchon, featuring Native Americans and frontier folk, ripped bodices, naval warfare, conspiracies erotic and political, major caffeine abuse. We follow the mismatch\'d pair--one rollicking, the other depressive; one Gothic, the other pre-Romantic--from their first journey together to the Cape of Good Hope, to pre-Revolutionary America and No reference to the present day is intended or should be inferred.\n\nThe sizable cast of characters includes anarchists, balloonists, gamblers, corporate tycoons, drug enthusiasts, innocents and decadents, mathematicians, mad scientists, shamans, psychics, and stage magicians, spies, detectives, adventuresses, and hired guns. There are cameo appearances by Nikola Tesla, Bela Lugosi, and Groucho Marx.\n\nAs an era of certainty comes crashing down around their ears and an unpredictable future commences, these folks are mostly just trying to pursue their lives. Sometimes they manage to catch up; sometimes it\'s their lives that pursue them.\n\nMeanwhile, the author is up to his usual business. Characters stop what they\'re doing to sing what are for the most part stupid songs. Strange sexual practices take place. Obscure languages are spoken, not always idiomatically. Contrary-to-the-fact occurrences occur. If it is not the world, it is what the world might be with a minor adjustment or two. According to some, this is one of the main purposes of fiction.\n\nLet the reader decide, let the reader beware. Good luck.\n\n--Thomas Pynchon\n\nAbout the Author:Thomas Pynchon is the author of V., The Crying of Lot 49, Gravity\'s Rainbow, Slow Learner, a collection of short stories, Vineland and, most recently, Mason and Dixon. This David was not the humble shepherd who slew Goliath and became king, however, but was a usurper, adulterer, and murderer--a Middle Eastern despot of a familiar type. McKenzie shows that the story of humble beginnings is utterly misleading: &quot;shepherd&quot; is a metaphor for &quot;king,&quot; and David came from a wealthy, upper-class background. Similarly, McKenzie reveals how David\'s ascent to power, traditionally attributed to popularity and divine blessing, in fact resulted from a campaign of terror and assassination. While instituting a full-blown Middle Eastern monarchy, David was an aggressive leader, a devious politician, and a ruthless war chief. Throughout his scandalous reign, important figures who stood in his way died at convenient times, under questionable circumstances. Even his own sons were not spared. Soon it was gone, only to be succeeded by another. And another. Strange, Karinthy thought, it had been years since Budapest had streetcars. Only then did he realize he was suffering from an auditory hallucination of extraordinary intensity. What in fact Karinthy was suffering from was a brain tumor, not cancerous but hardly benign, though it was only much later—after spells of giddiness, fainting fits, friends remarking that his handwriting had altered, and books going blank before his eyes—that he consulted a doctor and embarked on a series of examinations that would lead to brain surgery. Karinthy’s description of his descent into illness and his observations of his symptoms, thoughts, and feelings, as well as of his friends’ and doctors’ varied responses to his predicament, are exact and engrossing and entirely free of self-pity. A Journey Round My Skull is not only an extraordinary piece of medical testimony, but a powerful work of literature—one that dances brilliantly on the edge of extinction.<\/span>\n  <a <span id="freeTextContainer12443173230491124714"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces' rel='nofollow noopener'>See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br/><br/>See also:<br/>Serbian: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18609218.Tomas_Man' title='Tomas Man' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tomas Man</a><br/><br/>Thomas Mann was a German novelist, short story writer, social critic, philanthropist, essayist, and Nobel Prize laureate in 1929, known for his series of highly symbolic and ironic epic novels and novellas, noted for their insight into the psycholo</span>
  <span id="freeText12443173230491124714" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the GoodReads database with this name. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show_group/44217.Master_List_of_Author_Disambiguation_by_Spaces" rel="nofollow noopener">See this thread for more information</a>.</i><br /><br />See also:<br />Serbian: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18609218.Tomas_Man" title="Tomas Man" rel="nofollow noopener">Tomas Man</a><br /><br />Thomas Mann was a German novelist, short story writer, social critic, philanthropist, essayist, and Nobel Prize laureate in 1929, known for his series of highly symbolic and ironic epic novels and novellas, noted for their insight into the psychology of the artist and the intellectual. His analysis and critique of the European and German soul used modernized German and Biblical stories, as well as the ideas of Goethe, Nietzsche, and Schopenhauer. His older brother was the radical writer Heinrich Mann, and three of his six children, Erika Mann, Klaus Mann and Golo Mann, also became important German writers. When Hitler came to power in 1933, Mann fled to Switzerland. When World War II broke out in 1939, he emigrated to the United States, from where he returned to Switzerland in 1952. Thomas Mann is one of the best-known exponents of the so-called Exilliteratur.</span>

Deploying lavish, persuasive detail, Mann conjures for us the world of patriarchs and pharaohs, the ancient civilizations of Egypt, Mesopotamia, and Palestine, and the universal force of human love in all its beauty, desperation, absurdity, and pain.
